b^a ≡ f(b)^{f(a)} (mod f(a)).

For example, if f(n) > 2n, then for b = 2, we have 2^n - 1 may be much smaller than f(n) but could still be divisible if f(n) is 1, but if f(n) is > 2^n - 1, then divisibility cannot hold unless 2^n - 1 = 0 (impossible). In particular, consider b = 1 gives 0, trivial. b = 2 gives 2^n - 1. b = 3 gives 3^n - 1, etc. So f(n) must divide each of these differences. That seems like f(n) must be a common divisor of all numbers (b^n - 1). The set of common divisors is limited; indeed, if f(n) divides all b^n - 1 for b ∈ N, then in particular it divides (2^n - 1) and (3^n - 1). The gcd of these numbers is something like 1 unless n has some special property. For example, if n = 1, then 2^1-1=1, 3^1-1=2, gcd=1. For n = 2, 2^2-1=3, 3^2-1=8, gcd=1. But the condition requires divisibility for all b, not just those coprime to d. So if d has a prime factor p, then consider b = p (multiple of p). Then b^n - 1 ≡ -1 mod p, so p does not divide b^n - 1. Therefore, any d > 1 cannot divide b^n - 1 for all b, because if we take b to be a multiple of a prime p dividing d, then b^n - 1 ≡ -1 mod p, not 0. Therefore, the only possible d that divides all b^n - 1 is d = 1. But note: The condition is f(n) divides b^n - f(b)^{f(n)}. If f(b) = 1 for all b, then indeed it becomes b^n - 1. Thus, to prove that f(n) ≤ 2n, we could try to prove that f(n) ≤ n for all n ≥ 3. That seems plausible if we can show that for any n ≥ 3, f(n) must divide n^n and also must satisfy some congruence that forces f(n) ≤ n. But we have a counterexample: f(2) = 4 > 2. So different primes.

The general idea: For any n ≥ 3, we can find a prime p such that p > 2n (or maybe p > n) and also p does not divide n, and then consider a = p and b = n. That gives f(p) | n^p - f(n)^{f(p)}. Since f(p) is a divisor of p^p, and p is large, f(p) is likely small. But we don't know f(p). However, we can also consider a = n and b = p, which gives f(n) | p^n - f(p)^{f(n)}. That's a relation involving f(n) and f(p). If we can prove that for large p, f(p) is 1 or something? Not.

Alternatively, we can use a = n and b = some number that is a multiple of f(n) and also a prime? That doesn't give bound.

Let's step back. The problem is to determine the smallest constant c such that f(n) ≤ c n for all bonza functions f and all n. We have a function with f(2)=4, so c must be at least 2. If we can prove that for any bonza function, f(n) ≤ 2n, then c=2. But we need to confirm that there is no bonza function with f(n) > 2n for some n. Our earlier attempt to construct a function with f(3)=9 fails, as shown. Could we have a function with f(2)=4 (already ≤2n), f(3)=3 (≤2n), f(4)=4 (≤2n), f(5)=5 (≤2n), f(6)=? we suspect ≤6. So indeed f(n) ≤ 2n holds. But we need a proof. The proof likely uses the condition with a = 2 and b = n to bound f(n) in terms of n. Let's explore that.

We have f(2) | n^2 - f(n)^{f(2)}. Since f(2) is 1,2,4. Let's denote d = f(2). Then for any n, we have:

d | n^2 - f(n)^d.

Now, if d=2, then 2 | n^2 - f(n)^2. This implies that n and f(n) have the same parity. That's a condition. If d=4, then 4 | n^2 - f(n)^4. This imposes more. So we can try to bound f(n) using the fact that if f(n) is large, then f(n)^d is huge, so the congruence condition is easy to satisfy. So not.

Maybe we can use the condition with a = n and b = 1 to get f(n) | 1 - 1 = 0, trivial. With a = n and b = 2, we get f(n) | 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}. Since f(2) is small, this gives a relation that might limit f(n). Suppose f(n) > 2n. Then consider the valuation v2(f(n)). Since f(n) divides 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}. If f(2)=2, then 2^n - 2^{f(n)}. If f(n) is large, then 2^{f(n)} is huge, so the difference is negative large. But we can consider the p-adic valuations. For v2, 2^n - 2^{f(n)} = 2^n (1 - 2^{f(n)-n}). If f(n) > n, then the second term is negative. The 2-adic valuation of this difference is n + v2(1 - 2^{f(n)-n})? But 1 - 2^{k} is odd when k>0, because 2^{k} is even, 1 - even is odd. We can try to characterize: If x is multiple of 3, then x^9 ≡ 0 (mod 9). If x is not multiple of 3, then x^6 ≡ 1 (mod 9) (by Euler's theorem because φ(9)=6, and gcd(x,9)=1). Then x^9 = x^6 * x^3 ≡ x^3 (mod 9). So x^9 ≡ x^3 (mod 9) for x coprime to 9. Thus the condition 9 | b^3 - f(b)^9 becomes:

- If f(b) is multiple of 3, then f(b)^9 ≡ 0 → need b^3 ≡ 0 (mod 9) → b multiple of 3.
- If f(b) is not multiple of 3, then f(b)^9 ≡ f(b)^3 → need b^3 ≡ f(b)^3 (mod 9). Since cubes mod 9 are 0,1,8, this imposes that f(b) and b have same cube residue. We want to find for which n does the following hold: For all b ∈ ℕ, n^2 divides b^n - b^{n^2}. This is equivalent to: For all b, b^{n^2} ≡ b^n (mod n^2). Consider the prime factorization of n. Now, b^n - b^{n^2} = b^n (b^{n(n-1)} - 1). So v_p(b^n) + v_p(b^{n(n-1)} - 1) ≥ 2α. Since b^n may contain p-adic factors. This is a bit messy.

But maybe we don't need to characterize all n for which the property holds. Our goal is to determine the smallest constant c such that f(n) ≤ c n for all bonza functions f. We want to know for which n can we have f(n) = n^2 (or larger) and keep f(m)=m for m≠n. Let's define a candidate function g_n as:

Now, b^n - b^{n^2} = b^n (b^{n(n-1)} - 1). So v_p(b^n) + v_p(b^{n(n-1)} - 1) ≥ 2α.

Since f(6) divides 6^6 = 46656, k must be a divisor of 46656. The divisors are of the form 2^a * 3^b with 0 ≤ a,b ≤ 6. So k = 2^a * 3^b with a,b ∈ {0,...,6}. We have k > 6. So possible values are 9,12,18,24,27,36,48,54,64? Wait 64 is 2^6, but 64 is not a divisor of 46656? 46656 / 64 = 729, which is 3^6, so yes 64 is a divisor (2^6 * 3^0). So k can be 9 (3^2), 12 (2^2*3), 18 (2*3^2), 24 (2^3*3), 27 (3^3), 36 (2^2*3^2), 48 (2^4*3), 54 (2*3^3), 64 (2^6), 72 (2^3*3^2), 81? 81 is 3^4, but 81 is not a divisor of 46656 because 46656 / 81 = 576, which is 2^6 * 3^2? Actually 81 = 3^4. 46656 / 81 = 576, which is 2^6 * 3^2, so yes 81 is a divisor? Wait 46656 = 2^6 * 3^6. Dividing by 3^4 gives 2^6 * 3^2 = 64 * 9 = 576, integer. So 81 is a divisor (3^4). So 81 is allowed. So many values.

Now, we also have constraints from a=2,b=6: f(2) divides 36 - f(6)^{f(2)}. Since f(2) must be 2 (as argued), we have 2 divides 36 - k^2. So k must be even. That's a condition. So k must be even. So candidates with even k: 12,18,24,36,48,54,64,72,81? 81 is odd, so not. 9 is odd. So even k are those with a≥1. So k is a multiple of 2. So k is even.

Now, a=3,b=6: f(3)=3 divides 216 - k^3. So k^3 ≡ 0 mod 3 → k multiple of 3. So k must be a multiple of 3. So k must be a multiple of 6. So k is divisible by 6. etc. But we need to consider the fact that f(6) must also satisfy a=6,b=2 and a=6,b=3, etc. We saw that if f(6) is large, then a=6,b=2 fails. So maybe only f(6) = 6 works. But we need to prove that any bonza function must have f(6) ≤ 6. Let's attempt to prove that.

Consider a = 6 and b = 2. Then f(6) | 2^6 - f(2)^{f(6)}. Since f(2) = 2, this becomes f(6) | 64 - 2^{f(6)}. We want to show that if f(6) > 6, then 64 - 2^{f(6)} is not a multiple of f(6). Equivalently, 2^{f(6)} ≠ 64 (mod f(6)). But we can analyze the valuation. Note that f(6) divides 64 - 2^{f(6)}. Since f(6) is a divisor of 6^6, it's of the form 2^a * 3^b. Let's denote k = f(6). Then 2^k ≡ 64 (mod k). Also, k divides 6^6.

Now, if k is even (it must be, from a=2,b=6), then v2(k) ≥ 1. If k is a multiple of 3, then v3(k) ≥ 1. So k is a multiple of 6. So k = 6 * m where m is some integer. Since k divides 6^6, m must be of the form 2^a * 3^b with a,b ≤ 5? Actually 6^6 = 2^6 * 3^6. If k = 2^a * 3^b, then a ≤ 6, b ≤ 6. k being a multiple of 6 means a ≥ 1 and b ≥ 1. So a,b ≥ 1. So k = 2^a * 3^b with a,b ≥ 1.

Now, we have 2^k ≡ 64 (mod k). Since k is even, 2^k is divisible by 2^k, so v2(2^k) = k. So 2^k is a huge power of 2. Mod k, it's something. But maybe we can use LTE on 64 - 2^k. Write 2^k - 64 = 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1). So 2^k - 64 = 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1). Then 64 - 2^k = -(2^k - 64). So we have k divides 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1). Since k is a multiple of 6, it might have factors of 2 and 3. The factor 2^6 is 64. So we have:

k | 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1). Since k is a divisor of 2^6 * 3^6 (with a,b≥1), we can compare.

Now, if k > 64, then 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1) is huge. But we need to check whether k can divide that. Since k is composed only of primes 2 and 3, and 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1) is not obviously a multiple of all powers of 2 and 3 that appear in k. Let's analyze the p-adic valuations.

We want to find all k = 2^a * 3^b (a,b ≥ 1, a ≤ 6, b ≤ 6) such that k divides 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1). That's a condition. But maybe we can prove that the only k that satisfy this and also the other conditions (like from a=5,b=6) is k = 6. However, we saw that a=5,b=6 gives k ≡ 1 (mod 5). So we need k ≡ 1 mod 5. That's a restriction. So the candidate k must satisfy:

- k = 2^a * 3^b, a,b ≥ 1, a ≤ 6, b ≤ 6.
- k ≡ 1 (mod 5) (from a=5,b=6)
- k ≡ 6 (mod 7) (from a=7,b=6)
- k divides 2^6 (2^{k-6} - 1) (from a=6,b=2)

But we also have a=6,b=3 gives k multiple of 3, which is satisfied. a=6,b=2 gives the condition. a=6,b=1 trivial. a=6,b=7? That's a=6,b=7 gives f(6) | 7^6 - f(7)^{f(6)}. That's another condition. But maybe we can prove that f(6) ≤ 6 by contradiction using a=6,b=2. Let's attempt to prove: If f(6) > 6, then f(6) must be even and multiple of 3. So f(6) ∈ {12,18,24,30,36,42,48,54,60,66,72,78,84,90,96,102,108,114,120,126,132,138,144,150,156,162,168,174,180,186,192,198,204,210,216,222,228,234,240,246,252,258,264,270,276,282,288,294,300,306,312,318,324,330,336,342,348,354,360,366,372,378,384,390,396,402,408,414,420,426,432,438,444,450,456,462,468,474,480,486,492,498,504,510,516,522,528,534,540,546,552,558,564,570,576,582,588,594,600,606,612,618,624,630,636,642,648,654,660,666,672,678,684,690,696,702,708,714,720,726,732,738,744,750,756,762,768,774,780,786,792,798,804,810,816,822,828,834,840,846,852,858,864,870,876,882,888,894,900,906,912,918,924,930,936,942,948,954,960,966,972,978,984,990,996, etc. But also must be a divisor of 6^6=46656. So the maximum possible k is 46656 itself, but 46656 = 2^6 * 3^6. So k can be any divisor. So we have many candidates. Now, we need to see which of these satisfy the divisibility by f(6) from a=6,b=2. Let's compute for each candidate the residue of 2^k mod k. But maybe we can derive a bound: Since k | 64 - 2^k, we have 2^k ≡ 64 (mod k). If k > 64, then 2^k is much larger than k. However, note that 2^k mod k is not necessarily 0. But maybe we can show that if k > 64, then 2^k mod k is at least something, but we need it to be 64. Actually we need k to divide 64 - 2^k. That's equivalent to 2^k ≡ 64 (mod k). For k > 64, we have 2^k is huge. But we can consider the order modulo k if k is coprime to 2. But k is even, so not coprime. However, we can use the fact that 2^k is divisible by 2^k, so v2(2^k) = k. Since k is even, v2(k) ≤ k, obviously. For the divisibility, we need that the difference is a multiple of k. This is a strong condition.

Let's try to prove that if k > 6, then k cannot divide 64 - 2^k. But we need a rigorous proof. Let's test with k=12. 2^12=4096, 64-4096 = -4032. 4032/12 = 336, integer. So k=12 divides 64 - 2^12? 64 - 4096 = -4032, 4032/12 = 336, yes. So k=12 works with a=6,b=2. But we already saw that a=7,b=6 fails for k=12. So it's not that a=6,b=2 fails. Indeed, k=12 satisfies 12 | 64 - 2^12. So that's okay. So a=6,b=2 is not the restrictive one for k=12. The problem with k=12 was a=7,b=6. So we need to consider multiple constraints. The question is: can we find any k > 6 that satisfies all constraints? We need to prove that none exist. That would show that f(6) ≤ 6. But maybe some k > 6 could satisfy all constraints? Let's test with k=36. 2^36 is huge, 64 - 2^36 is negative huge. Does 36 divide that? Compute 2^36 mod 36. 36=4*9. 2^36 is divisible by 2^2=4, so 2^36 ≡ 0 mod 4. 2^36 mod 9: 2^6=64≡1, so 2^36 = (2^6)^6 ≡ 1. So 2^36 ≡ 1 mod 9. Then 64 - 2^36 ≡ 0 - 1 = -1 ≡ 8 mod 9, not 0. So 36 does not divide. So k=36 fails a=6,b=2. So k=36 is out. k=48? 48 = 2^4 * 3. 2^48 mod 48: 2^48 is divisible by 2^4=16, so v2 is high. 2^48 mod 3: 2^48 ≡ (2^2)^24 ≡ 4^24 ≡ 1^24 = 1 mod 3. So 2^48 ≡ 1 mod 3. Then 64 - 2^48 ≡ 0 - 1 = -1 mod 3, not 0. So fails. k=54? 54 = 2*3^3. 2^54 mod 2 is 0, but mod 27? 2^54 = (2^6)^9 = 64^9 ≡ 1^9 = 1 mod 9? Actually need to compute modulo 27. But we can use that 2^54 is divisible by 2, but we need to check divisibility by 54, which is 2*27. 2^54 is a power of 2, so v2=54. So it's divisible by 2. For v3, 2^54 is not divisible by 3, so 2^54 mod 27 is something. 2^54 mod 27: φ(27)=18, 2^18 ≡ 1, so 2^54 = (2^18)^3 ≡ 1. So 2^54 ≡ 1 mod 27. Then 64 - 2^54 ≡ 64 - 1 = 63 mod 27 ≡ 63 - 2*27 = 9. So not divisible by 27. So fails. k=64? 64 is a divisor, but 64 is not a multiple of 3 (so fails a=3,b=6). But we are considering constraints: a=3,b=6 requires k multiple of 3. We need to determine the smallest constant c such that f(n) ≤ c n for all bonza functions f and all n. The problem may have a nontrivial answer. We should analyze the condition thoroughly.

Given f: N → N is bonza if for all a,b:

f(a) divides b^a - f(b)^{f(a)}.   (1)

We can try to find constraints on f(n). For a fixed a, we can choose b such that f(b) is 1 (like b=1), then we get f(a) divides 1^a - 1^{f(a)} = 0, trivial. So that doesn't restrict f(a). Compute n^3 mod 9. As before, residues are 0,1,8. For any integer x, x^9 mod 9 = x^3 mod 9 if gcd(x,9)=1, else 0 if x multiple of 3. So we need:

- If n^3 ≡ 0 mod 9, then n is multiple of 3. Then we need f(n)^9 ≡ 0 mod 9 → f(n) multiple of 3.
- If n^3 ≡ 1 mod 9, then we need f(n)^9 ≡ 1 mod 9. This implies f(n) ≡ 1 mod 3? Actually if f(n) is not multiple of 3, then f(n)^9 ≡ f(n)^3 mod 9. So we need f(n)^3 ≡ 1 mod 9. The cubic residues mod 9 for numbers coprime to 3 are 1 and 8. 1^3=1, 2^3=8, 4^3=64≡1, 5^3=125≡8, 7^3=343≡1, 8^3=512≡8. So f(n)^3 ≡ 1 if f(n) ≡ 1,4,7 mod 9. If f(n) ≡ 2,5,8 mod 9, then f(n)^3 ≡ 8. - If n^3 ≡ 8 mod 9, then we need f(n)^9 ≡ 8 mod 9. For f(n) not multiple of 3, we need f(n)^3 ≡ 8 mod 9. That happens if f(n) ≡ 2,5,8 mod 9. So f(n) must be ≡ 2,5,8 mod 9. Also, if f(n) multiple of 3, then 0 ≠ 8.

Thus, for each n, f(n) must be congruent to a specific residue class mod 9 (depending on n mod 3). That's a restriction.

The divisibility imposes that any prime p dividing f(n) must divide 2^n - 2^{f(n)}. That's not obvious.

If f(n) > n, then consider v2: 2^n - 2^{f(n)} = 2^n (1 - 2^{f(n)-n}) has v2 = n. So f(n) must have v2 ≤ n. That's not restrictive. However, note that 2^n - 2^{f(n)} is negative, but its absolute value is 2^{f(n)} - 2^n. For f(n) > n, the exponent is larger. The divisibility implies that f(n) divides that number, so f(n) ≤ |2^n - 2^{f(n)}| = 2^{f(n)} - 2^n. That's huge. So not a bound.

Another idea: Use the condition with a = n and b = n (trivial). With a = n and b = 1 trivial. Suppose we have a bonza function f. For a prime p, we have f(p) | p^p. Also, from a=2,b=p: f(2) divides p^2 - f(p)^{f(2)}. That's a condition. And from a=p,b=2: f(p) divides 2^p - f(2)^{f(p)}. Possibly not. Let's try to see if there is any bonza function with f(p) = p^p. That would be huge. Then a=p,b=2 would give f(p) | 2^p - f(2)^{p^p}. That seems unlikely because 2^p - f(2)^{p^p} is huge negative, but its absolute value is roughly f(2)^{p^p}, which is astronomically larger than 2^p. The condition a=p,b=2 gives f(p) | 2^p - f(2)^{f(p)}. If f(p) is huge, then 2^p - f(2)^{f(p)} is negative huge, but its magnitude is about f(2)^{f(p)}. So f(p) dividing that is plausible. So f(p) is a power of p. So f(p) = p^k. So it's not as large as p^p, but could be p^p. That's allowed. So we can have f(p) = p^p (k=p). Then f(p) divides p^p. So it's a divisor. So that's allowed. ## Solution
We are given that $\mathbb{N}$ denotes the set of positive integers.  A function $f:\mathbb{N}\to\mathbb{N}$ is said to be **bonza** if  

\[
f(a)\mid b^{\,a}-f(b)^{\,f(a)}\qquad\text{for all }a,b\in\mathbb{N}.
\]

We need to determine the smallest real constant $c$ such that $f(n)\le c\,n$ holds for every bonza function $f$ and every positive integer $n$.

We will prove that $c=2$ is the optimal constant.  That is:

* (1) $c=2$ works: for every bonza function $f$ and every $n\in\mathbb{N}$ we have $f(n)\le 2n$.
* (2) $c$ cannot be smaller than $2$ because there exists a bonza function for which $f(2)=2$ (so $f(2)/2=1$) but we need a function with $f(n)/n>1$ for some $n$ to push $c$ above $1$.  Actually we need to show that $2$ is the smallest constant, i.e. there is no constant $c<2$ that works for all bonza functions.  We will exhibit a bonza function with $f(n)=n$ for all $n$; then $f(n)/n=1$, which is $\le c$ for $c\ge1$, so that doesn't force $c\ge2$.  But we need a function that violates $c<2$, i.e., for some $n$ we have $f(n)/n > c$ for all $c<2$.  That would be $f(2)=4$ which we proved is impossible.  Actually we need to find the supremum of $f(n)/n$ over all bonza functions and all $n$.  If we can prove that $f(n)\le n$ for all $n\ge1$ and all bonza $f$, then $c=1$ would suffice.  But is it true?  The function $f(n)=n$ is bonza, and $f(2)=2$ gives ratio $1$.  So $c=1$ would work.  But the problem asks to determine the smallest constant $c$ such that $f(n)\le c n$ for **all** bonza functions $f$ and all $n$.  If $c=1$ works, then it's the answer.  However, we need to verify whether any bonza function can have $f(n)>n$ for some $n$.  We've seen that $f(3)=9$ is impossible, and $f(2)=4$ is impossible.  So perhaps $f(n)\le n$ for all $n$.  That would give $c=1$.  But is that always true?  Let's test $f(4)=8$ fails, $f(6)=12$ fails.  So maybe $f(n)\le n$ for all $n$.  Then the constant $c=1$ is the smallest.  But we need to confirm with $n=2$, $f(2)=2$ (≤2).  So $c=1$ works.  However, the problem might have a different answer.  Let's analyze more carefully.

We need to determine the supremum of $f(n)/n$ over all bonza functions $f$ and all $n$.  We have already derived that $f(2)=2$ (forced), $f(3)=3$ (forced) and $f(4)=4$ (forced) etc.  But is it forced that $f(5)=5$?  Let's check using a=7,b=5.  That gives $f(7)$ divides $5^7 - f(5)^{f(7)}$.  Not directly.  But we can use the pair (a=5,b=3) and (a=3,b=5) to bound $f(5)$.  We need to systematically prove that for all $n\ge 3$, $f(n)\le n$.  Let's attempt to prove by induction.

We have $f(1)=1$ (trivial).  Show $f(2)=2$ (already done).  Now assume $f(k)\le k$ for all $k<n$.  Want $f(n)\le n$.  Use the condition with $a=n$ and $b=2$: $f(n)\mid 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}=2^n - 2^{f(n)}$.  So $f(n)$ divides $2^n - 2^{f(n)}$.  If $f(n)>n$, then $2^{f(n)}>2^n$, so $2^n - 2^{f(n)}$ is negative.  Its absolute value is $2^{f(n)} - 2^n$.  We can consider the 2-adic valuation.  $v_2(2^n - 2^{f(n)}) = n$ because $2^n(1 - 2^{f(n)-n})$ and $1 - 2^{f(n)-n}$ is odd.  Therefore $f(n)$ has $v_2 \le n$.  That's not enough.

Consider $a=2$ and $b=n$: $f(2)\mid n^2 - f(n)^{f(2)}$, i.e. $2\mid n^2 - f(n)^2$.  This forces $f(n)$ to have the same parity as $n$.  That's a condition.

Now, note that $f(n)\mid n^n$.  So $f(n)$ is composed only of primes dividing $n$.  Let $n$ have prime factorization $n = \prod p_i^{e_i}$.  Then $f(n) = \prod p_i^{a_i}$ with $0 \le a_i \le n\,e_i$ (since $v_{p_i}(n^n) = n\,v_{p_i}(n)$).  So $f(n)$ could be huge.

The above suggests that the only way to get an upper bound is to use the condition with a specific small $a$ (like $a = n+1$ or a prime) and $b = n$, combined with the fact that $f(a)$ must satisfy some congruences that force $f(n)$ to be $n$.  For $n=3$, we used $a=5$ and $a=7$ to get $f(3)=3$.  For $n=4$, we used $a=5$ and $a=7$ to get $f(4)=4$.  For $n=5$, we can use $a=7$ and $a=11$?  But we need to prove that for any $n$, there exists a prime $p$ such that $p > n$ and $p \nmid n$, and then the condition $a = p$, $b = n$ yields a congruence on $f(n)$ modulo $p$ (or something).  Actually we saw that for $n=3$, $a=5$ and $a=7$ forced $f(3)=3$ because the only divisors of $27$ that satisfy $f(3)\equiv 3 \pmod 5$ and $f(3)\equiv 3 \pmod 7$ are $3$ (and $1$? Actually $1$ mod $5$ is $1$, not $3$; $27$ mod $5$ is $2$; $9$ mod $5$ is $4$; $3$ mod $5$ is $3$; $27$ mod $7$ is $6$; $9$ mod $7$ is $2$; $3$ mod $7$ is $3$).  So $f(3)=3$ is forced.  For $n=4$, we used $a=5$ and $a=7$ to force $f(4)=4$.  So the method is: choose two distinct primes $p,q$ both larger than $n$ and such that $p,q$ do not divide $n$ (they can be any primes > n).  Then the condition $a = p$, $b = n$ gives $f(p) \mid n^p - f(n)^{f(p)}$.  That's not a simple congruence on $f(n)$.  But the condition $a = n$, $b = p$ gives $f(n) \mid p^n - f(p)^{f(n)}$.  That's also not simple.

However, we used a different relation: we considered $a = p$ (prime) and $b = n$, and then we used the fact that $f(p)$ must be a divisor of $p^p$, and also $f(p)$ must satisfy the condition with $a=2$ and $b=p$ maybe.  But we didn't need $f(p)$.  Let's revisit how we deduced $f(3)=3$ and $f(4)=4$.

We had:

For $n=3$: from $a=3,b=2$ we got $f(3) \mid 8 - f(2)^3$.  From $a=2,b=3$ we got $f(2) \mid 9 - f(3)^2$.  With $f(2)=2$ (forced by earlier argument? Actually we derived $f(2)=2$ from the system (2,3) constraints and the condition $a=3,b=2$ combined with $a=2,b=3$? Let's compute systematically.  We need to find all possible pairs $(f(2),f(3))$ that satisfy these necessary conditions.  Then we also have the condition with $a=7,b=3$ which we used to bound $f(3)$?  Actually we used $a=7,b=3$ to get $7 \mid 3^7 - f(3)^7$?  That's not correct.  The correct condition is $f(7) \mid 3^7 - f(3)^{f(7)}$.  That doesn't involve $f(3)^7$ unless $f(7)=7$.  So we cannot conclude $7 \mid 3^7 - f(3)^7$ unless $f(7)=7$ is known.  But we can use a different prime like $5$ for $n=3$?  For $n=3$, $a=5$ and $b=3$ gives $f(5) \mid 3^5 - f(3)^{f(5)}$.  Not helpful.  So the method we used earlier (a=7,b=3) was wrong.  So we need to reexamine.

Let's think again.  The condition is:

\[
f(a) \mid b^a - f(b)^{f(a)} \qquad \forall a,b.
\]

If we set $b = a$, we get $f(a) \mid a^a$.  So $f(a)$ divides $a^a$.  That's necessary.

Now, to get an upper bound on $f(n)$ like $f(n) \le 2n$, perhaps we can use the condition with $a = n$ and $b = 2$ to get $f(n) \mid 2^n - f(2)^{f(n)}$.  Since $f(2)$ is small, this forces $f(n)$ to be small in some sense.  Let's try to bound $f(n)$ by $2n$ using this.

We have $f(2) \mid 4$, so $f(2) \in \{1,2,4\}$.  We'll consider each case and see what constraints arise.

**Case $f(2)=1$.**  Then from $a=2,b=n$ we get $1 \mid n^2 - f(n)^1$ (trivial).  From $a=n,b=2$ we get $f(n) \mid 2^n - 1^{f(n)} = 2^n - 1$.  So $f(n) \mid 2^n - 1$.  Also $f(n) \mid n^n$.  This is a system.  Does this force $f(n) \le n$?  Possibly $f(n)$ could be large, but it must divide $2^n - 1$, which is much smaller than $n^n$ for large $n$.  In fact, $2^n - 1$ grows roughly like $2^n$, so $f(n) \le 2^n - 1$.  That's not linear.  But we need to prove $f(n) \le 2n$.  Is it possible to have $f(n) = 2^n - 1$?  But also $f(n)$ must divide $n^n$.  So $f(n)$ cannot be $2^n - 1$ unless $2^n - 1$ divides $n^n$.  That seems unlikely for $n>2$.  So we need a stronger bound.  The condition $f(n) \mid 2^n - 1$ gives $f(n) \le 2^n - 1$.  That's huge, but maybe combined with $f(n) \mid n^n$, we can get that $f(n)$ is a divisor of $\gcd(n^n, 2^n - 1)$.  Since $n^n$ and $2^n - 1$ are often coprime (or have small gcd).  For $n$ odd, $2^n - 1$ is odd, $n^n$ is odd if $n$ is odd, but they could share some odd primes.  For $n=3$, $\gcd(27, 7) = 1$, so $f(3)=1$ forced (if $f(2)=1$).  For $n=5$, $\gcd(3125, 31) = 1$, so $f(5)=1$.  So indeed, $f(n)=1$ for all $n$ (since $f(n)$ must divide both $n^n$ and $2^n-1$, and the gcd is usually $1$ for many $n$).  But could $f(n)$ be $>1$ for some $n$?  For $n=1$, trivial.  For $n=2$, $2^2-1=3$, $\gcd(4,3)=1$, so $f(2)=1$ as well.  So if $f(2)=1$, then $f(n)=1$ for all $n$?  Not necessarily, because the condition $f(n) \mid 2^n - 1$ must hold for all $n$.  If $f(n)$ is a divisor of $2^n-1$, then $f(n) \le 2^n-1$.  Also $f(n) \mid n^n$.  So $f(n)$ divides $\gcd(n^n, 2^n-1)$.  For many $n$, this gcd is $1$ (e.g., $n=3$, $4$, $5$, $6$, $7$?).  But we need to check $n=6$: $2^6-1=63$, $\gcd(6^6=46656, 63) = 9$?  Actually $46656 / 9 = 5184$, $63/9=7$, so $\gcd=9$.  So $f(6)$ could be $3$ or $9$ or $1$ if $f(2)=1$.  But also $f(6)$ must divide $6^6=46656$ and $63$.  So $f(6) \mid 9$.  So $f(6) \in \{1,3,9\}$.  That's $\le 9$ which is $< 2n=12$.  So still $\le n$?  $9 > 6$, but it's $\le 9$.  So $f(6)$ could be $9$, which is $>6$, but $9 \le 2n=12$.  So $c=2$ might be needed.  But we also have the condition from $a=2,b=6$: $f(2)=1$ divides $36 - f(6)^1$, trivial.  So $f(6)=9$ is allowed?  But we must also check $a=6,b=2$: $f(6) \mid 2^6 - 1^{f(6)} = 64 - 1 = 63$.  So $f(6)$ must divide $63$.  $9$ divides $63$, yes.  So $f(6)=9$ is possible.  Then we need to define $f$ for all $n$ consistently.  The condition $f(6)=9$ would impose that $9 \mid n^3 - f(n)^9$ for all $n$ (since $a=6$, $b=n$ gives $f(6) \mid n^6 - f(n)^{f(6)} = n^6 - f(n)^9$).  But that's $9$ dividing $n^6 - f(n)^9$.  That's a restriction.  However, we can try to define $f$ for all $n$ to satisfy all constraints.  The existence of such a function is not obvious.  But we can attempt to construct a function where $f(2)=1$ and $f(6)=9$ and $f(1)=1$.  Then we need to assign $f(3)$, $f(4)$, $f(5)$, etc.  Let's see if we can find a consistent assignment.  For $n=3$, from $a=3,b=2$: $f(3) \mid 8 - f(2)^3 = 7$.  So $f(3) \mid 7$.  Also $f(3) \mid 27$.  So $f(3) \in \{1,7\}$.  $7$ is not a divisor of $27$, so $f(3)=1$.  So $f(3)=1$.  Then $a=3,b=6$: $f(3)=1$ divides $6^3 - f(6)^1 = 216 - 9 = 207$, trivial.  $a=6,b=3$: $f(6)=9$ divides $3^6 - f(3)^9 = 729 - 1^9 = 728$.  $728/9$ is not integer.  So $f(6)=9$ fails with $a=6,b=3$.  Thus, if $f(2)=1$, we cannot have $f(6)=9$ because $a=6,b=3$ would violate.  So $f(6)$ must be a divisor of $3^6-1=728$ as well?  Wait condition $a=6,b=3$: $f(6) \mid 3^6 - f(3)^{f(6)}$.  With $f(3)=1$, that's $729 - 1 = 728$.  So $f(6) \mid 728$.  $728 = 2^3 * 7 * 13$.  So $f(6)$ must divide $728$.  Also $f(6) \mid 6^6 = 46656$.  So $f(6) \mid \gcd(46656, 728)$.  Compute $\gcd(46656, 728)$.  $728*64 = 46672$, too high; $728*63 = 45864$, remainder 792.  $792/728$? Actually we can compute gcd using Euclidean algorithm: $46656 \mod 728 = 46656 - 728*64 = 46656 - 46592 = 64$.  So $\gcd(46656,728) = \gcd(728,64) = 8$?  $728 \mod 64 = 728 - 64*11 = 728-704=24$, $64 \mod 24 = 64-24*2=16$, $24 \mod 16 = 8$, $16 \mod 8 = 0$, so gcd=8.  So $f(6) \mid 8$.  Thus $f(6) \in \{1,2,4,8\}$.  But also $f(6) \mid 6^6$ and $f(6)$ must be a divisor of $728$ (from $a=6,b=3$).  So $f(6) \mid 8$.  That's possible.  So $f(6)$ could be $8$, which is $>6$.  So with $f(2)=1$, $f(6)=8$ is allowed?  Let's test $a=6,b=2$: $f(6)=8$ divides $2^6 - f(2)^{8}=64-1=63$, $8$ does not divide $63$.  So fails.  Actually condition $a=6,b=2$: $f(6) \mid 2^6 - f(2)^{f(6)} = 64 - 1^{8} = 63$.  So $8$ must divide $63$, false.  So $f(6)=8$ is impossible.  So $f(6)$ must be a divisor of $63$ as well.  $f(6) \mid 63$ from $a=6,b=2$ (since $f(2)=1$).  $63 = 3^2 * 7$.  So $f(6) \in \{1,3,7,9,21,63\}$.  Intersection with $\{1,2,4,8\}$ from earlier gcd is $\{1\}$.  So $f(6)=1$.  Thus, if $f(2)=1$, then $f(6)=1$.  So $f(6)$ cannot be large.  So $f(2)=1$ forces many values to be $1$.
